</p><p>During his final years, and especially after the death of Hephaestion, Alexander began to exhibit signs of <a href="/wiki/Megalomania" class="mw-redirect" title="Megalomania">megalomania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> His extraordinary achievements, coupled with his own ineffable sense of destiny and the flattery of his companions, may have combined to produce this effect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200720‚Äì21_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200720‚Äì21-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Delusions_of_grandeur" class="mw-redirect" title="Delusions of grandeur">delusions of grandeur</a> are readily visible in his <a href="#Will">will</a> and in his desire to conquer the world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> in as much as he is by various sources described as having <i>boundless ambition</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> an epithet, the meaning of which has descended into an historical clich√©.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He appears to have believed himself a deity, or at least sought to deify himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Olympias always insisted to him that he was the son of Zeus,<sup id="cite_ref-PA3_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA3-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> a theory apparently confirmed to him by the oracle of Amun at <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-P27_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P27-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> He began to identify himself as the son of Zeus-Ammon.<sup id="cite_ref-P27_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P27-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Alexander adopted elements of Persian dress and customs at court, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, a practice of which Macedonians disapproved, and were loath to perform.<sup id="cite_ref-AVII11_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVII11-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This behaviour cost him the sympathies of many of his countrymen.<sup id="cite_ref-PA45_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA45-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> However, Alexander also was a pragmatic ruler who understood the difficulties of ruling culturally disparate peoples, many of whom lived in kingdoms where the king was divine.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Thus, rather than megalomania, his behaviour may simply have been a practical attempt at strengthening his rule and keeping his empire together.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_relationships">Personal relationships</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personal_relationships_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Personal relationships of Alexander the Great">Personal relationships of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fresco_Alexander_and_Stateira.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Fresco_Alexander_and_Stateira.jpg/220px-Fresco_Alexander_and_Stateira.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Fresco_Alexander_and_Stateira.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fresco_Alexander_and_Stateira.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mural in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, depicting the marriage of Alexander to <a href="/wiki/Barsine" title="Barsine">Barsine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stateira_II" title="Stateira II">Stateira</a>) in 324 BC; the couple are apparently dressed as <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander married three times: <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a>, daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> nobleman <a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> out of love;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919&#91;httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990102433Achapter3D47_LXVII,_1&#93;_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990102433Achapter3D47_LXVII,_1]-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and the Persian princesses <a href="/wiki/Stateira_II" title="Stateira II">Stateira II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parysatis_II" title="Parysatis II">Parysatis II</a>, the former a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> and latter a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>, for political reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Carney_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carney-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936&#91;httppenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsPlutarchMoraliaFortuna_Alexandri2html_II,_6&#93;_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936[httppenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsPlutarchMoraliaFortuna_Alexandri2html_II,_6]-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> He apparently had two sons, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV of Macedon</a> by Roxana and, possibly, <a href="/wiki/Heracles_of_Macedon" title="Heracles of Macedon">Heracles of Macedon</a> from his mistress <a href="/wiki/Barsine" title="Barsine">Barsine</a>. He lost another child when Roxana miscarried at Babylon.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault2001100_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault2001100-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander also had a close relationship with his friend, general, and bodyguard <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, the son of a Macedonian noble.<sup id="cite_ref-AVII14_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16_182-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DSXVII114_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII114-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Hephaestion's death devastated Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-AVII14_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-P72_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P72-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> This event may have contributed to Alexander's failing health and detached <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental state</a> during his final months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723‚Äì24-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMD_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander's sexuality has been the subject of speculation and controversy in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The Roman era writer <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus" title="Athenaeus">Athenaeus</a> says, based on the scholar <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a>, who was Alexander's contemporary, that the king "was quite excessively keen on boys", and that Alexander sexually embraced his eunuch Bagoas in public.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> This episode is also told by Plutarch, probably based on the same source. None of Alexander's contemporaries, however, are known to have explicitly described Alexander's relationship with Hephaestion as sexual, though the pair was often compared to <a href="/wiki/Achilles_and_Patroclus" title="Achilles and Patroclus">Achilles and Patroclus</a>, whom classical Greek culture painted as a couple. Aelian writes of Alexander's visit to <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> where "Alexander garlanded the tomb of Achilles, and Hephaestion that of <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>, the latter hinting that he was a beloved of Alexander, in just the same way as Patroclus was of Achilles."<sup id="cite_ref-AelXII7_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AelXII7-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Some modern historians (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Robin_Lane_Fox" title="Robin Lane Fox">Robin Lane Fox</a>) believe not only that Alexander's youthful relationship with Hephaestion was sexual, but that their sexual contacts may have continued into adulthood, which went against the social norms of at least some Greek cities, such as Athens,<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESacks199516_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESacks199516-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> though some modern researchers have tentatively proposed that Macedonia (or at least the Macedonian court) may have been more tolerant of homosexuality between adults.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Green argues that there is little evidence in ancient sources that Alexander had much carnal interest in women; he did not produce an heir until the very end of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16_182-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> However, Ogden calculates that Alexander, who impregnated his partners thrice in eight years, had a higher matrimonial record than his father at the same age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Two of these pregnancies ‚Äî Stateira's and Barsine's ‚Äî are of dubious legitimacy.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Diodorus Siculus, Alexander accumulated a harem in the style of Persian kings, but he used it rather sparingly,<sup id="cite_ref-DSXVII77_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII77-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> showing great self-control in "pleasures of the body".<sup id="cite_ref-AVII28_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVII28-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Plutarch described how Alexander was infatuated by Roxana while complimenting him on not forcing himself on her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Green suggested that, in the context of the period, Alexander formed quite strong friendships with women, including <a href="/wiki/Ada_of_Caria" title="Ada of Caria">Ada of Caria</a>, who adopted him, and even Darius' mother <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, who supposedly died from grief upon hearing of Alexander's death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16_182-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715‚Äì16-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/220px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/330px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/440px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1040" data-file-height="611" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hellenistic world view after Alexander: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_world_map" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient world map">ancient world map</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> (276‚Äì194&#160;BC), incorporating information from the campaigns of Alexander and his successors.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander's legacy extended beyond his military conquests. His campaigns greatly increased contacts and trade between East and West, and vast areas to the east were significantly exposed to Greek civilization and influence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Some of the cities he founded became major cultural centers, many surviving into the 21st century. His chroniclers recorded valuable information about the areas through which he marched, while the Greeks themselves got a sense of belonging to a world beyond the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_kingdoms">Hellenistic kingdoms</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/220px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/330px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/440px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="682" data-file-height="547" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plan of Alexandria <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;30&#160;BC</span></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander's most immediate legacy was the introduction of Macedonian rule to huge new swathes of Asia. At the time of his death, Alexander's empire covered some 5,200,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (2,000,000&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-uconn_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uconn-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> and was the largest state of its time. Many of these areas remained in Macedonian hands or under Greek influence for the next 200‚Äì300&#160;years. The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period#The_successors" title="Hellenistic period">successor states</a> that emerged were, at least initially, dominant forces, and these 300&#160;years are often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii‚Äìxix_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii‚Äìxix-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The eastern borders of Alexander's empire began to collapse even during his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724‚Äì26_167-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724‚Äì26-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> However, the power vacuum he left in the northwest of the Indian subcontinent directly gave rise to one of the most powerful Indian dynasties in history, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>. Taking advantage of this power vacuum, <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> (referred to in Greek sources as "Sandrokottos"), of relatively humble origin, took control of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, and with that power base proceeded to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Empire" title="Nanda Empire">Nanda Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay200182‚Äì85_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay200182‚Äì85-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_of_cities">Founding of cities</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_founded_by_Alexander_the_Great" title="List of cities founded by Alexander the Great">List of cities founded by Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Over the course of his conquests, Alexander founded some <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Alexandria (disambiguation)">twenty cities that bore his name</a>, most of them east of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> The first, and greatest, was <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt, which would become one of the leading Mediterranean cities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The cities' locations reflected trade routes as well as defensive positions. At first, the cities must have been inhospitable, little more than defensive garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_105-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Following Alexander's death, many Greeks who had settled there tried to return to Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_105-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> However, a century or so after Alexander's death, many of the Alexandrias were thriving, with elaborate public buildings and substantial populations that included both Greek and local peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_105-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding_of_temples">Funding of temples</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/220px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/330px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/440px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dedication of Alexander the Great to <a href="/wiki/Athena_Polias" class="mw-redirect" title="Athena Polias">Athena Polias</a> at <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a>, now housed in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a><sup id="cite_ref-Burn_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 334 BC, Alexander the Great donated funds for the completion of the new temple of <a href="/wiki/Athena_Polias" class="mw-redirect" title="Athena Polias">Athena Polias</a> in <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BritishMuseumTumblr_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseumTumblr-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BritishMuseum_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseum-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Priene_Inscription" title="Priene Inscription">inscription from the temple</a>, now housed in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, declares: "King Alexander dedicated [this temple] to Athena Polias."<sup id="cite_ref-Burn_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> This inscription is one of the few independent archaeological discoveries confirming an episode from Alexander's life.<sup id="cite_ref-Burn_230-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> The temple was designed by <a href="/wiki/Pythius_of_Priene" title="Pythius of Priene">Pytheos</a>, one of the architects of the <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burn_230-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BritishMuseumTumblr_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseumTumblr-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BritishMuseum_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseum-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization">Hellenization</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/220px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/330px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/440px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2321" data-file-height="1288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander's empire was the largest state of its time, covering approximately 5.2&#160;million square km.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Hellenization</i> was coined by the German historian <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gustav_Droysen" title="Johann Gustav Droysen">Johann Gustav Droysen</a> to denote the spread of Greek language, culture, and population into the former Persian empire after Alexander's conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii‚Äìxix_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii‚Äìxix-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> That this export took place is undoubted, and can be seen in the great Hellenistic cities of, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756‚Äì59_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756‚Äì59-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> (south of modern <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Alexander sought to insert Greek elements into <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Persian culture</a> and attempted to hybridize Greek and Persian culture. This culminated in his aspiration to homogenize the populations of Asia and Europe. However, his successors explicitly rejected such policies. Nevertheless, Hellenization occurred throughout the region, accompanied by a distinct and opposite 'Orientalization' of the successor states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200721,_56‚Äì59_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200721,_56‚Äì59-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The core of the Hellenistic culture promulgated by the conquests was essentially <a href="/wiki/Athenian" class="mw-redirect" title="Athenian">Athenian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> The close association of men from across Greece in Alexander's army directly led to the emergence of the largely <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a>-based "<a href="/wiki/Koine" class="mw-redirect" title="Koine">koine</a>", or "common" Greek dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Koine spread throughout the Hellenistic world, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of Hellenistic lands and eventually the ancestor of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">modern Greek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">town planning</a>, education, local government, and art current in the Hellenistic period were all based on Classical Greek ideals, evolving into distinct new forms commonly grouped as Hellenistic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756‚Äì59_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756‚Äì59-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Aspects of Hellenistic culture were still evident in the traditions of the Byzantine Empire in the mid-15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization_in_Central_Asia_and_India">Hellenization in Central Asia and India</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_art" title="Indo-Greek art">Indo-Greek art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/220px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="365" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/330px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/440px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1746" data-file-height="2894" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist style</a>, 1st to 2nd century AD, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, northern Pakistan. <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the most pronounced effects of Hellenization can be seen in Afghanistan and India, in the region of the relatively late-rising <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a> (250‚Äì125 BC) (in modern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a> (180 BC ‚Äì 10 AD) in modern Afghanistan and India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> trade routes, Hellenistic culture hybridized with Iranian and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> cultures. The cosmopolitan art and mythology of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (a region spanning the upper confluence of the Indus, Swat and Kabul rivers in modern Pakistan) of the ~3rd century BC to the ~5th century AD are most evident of the direct contact between Hellenistic civilization and South Asia, as are the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>, which directly mention the Greeks within Ashoka's dominion as converting to Buddhism and the reception of Buddhist emissaries by Ashoka's contemporaries in the Hellenistic world.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> influenced the development of Buddhism<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and created a culture of <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>. These Greco-Buddhist kingdoms sent some of the first Buddhist missionaries to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and Hellenistic Asia and Europe (<a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_monasticism" title="Greco-Buddhist monasticism">Greco-Buddhist monasticism</a>).
</p><p>Some of the first and most influential figurative portrayals of the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> appeared at this time, perhaps modeled on Greek statues of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> in the Greco-Buddhist style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Several Buddhist traditions may have been influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a>: the concept of <a href="/wiki/Boddhisatvas" class="mw-redirect" title="Boddhisatvas">Boddhisatvas</a> is reminiscent of Greek divine heroes,<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> <a href="/wiki/Offering_(Buddhism)" title="Offering (Buddhism)">ceremonial practices</a> (burning <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a>, gifts of flowers, and food placed on altars) are similar to those practiced by the ancient Greeks; however, similar practices were also observed amongst the native Indic culture. One Greek king, <a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander I</a>, probably became Buddhist, and was immortalized in <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist literature</a> as 'Milinda'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101‚Äì09-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> The process of Hellenization also spurred trade between the east and west.<sup id="cite_ref-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f._243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f.-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> For example, Greek astronomical instruments dating to the 3rd century BC were found in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ai_Khanoum" class="mw-redirect" title="Ai Khanoum">Ai Khanoum</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> while the Greek concept of a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_earth" class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical earth">spherical earth</a> surrounded by the spheres of planets eventually supplanted the long-standing Indian cosmological belief of a disc consisting of four continents grouped around a central mountain (Mount Meru) like the petals of a flower.<sup id="cite_ref-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f._243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f.-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hayashi08Aryabhata_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayashi08Aryabhata-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yavanajataka" title="Yavanajataka">Yavanajataka</a> (lit. Greek astronomical treatise) and <a href="/wiki/Paulisa_Siddhanta" title="Paulisa Siddhanta">Paulisa Siddhanta</a> texts depict the influence of Greek astronomical ideas on Indian astronomy.
</p><p>Following the conquests of Alexander the Great in the east, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_influence_on_Indian_art" title="Hellenistic influence on Indian art">Hellenistic influence on Indian art</a> was far-ranging. In the area of <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, a few examples of the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic order</a> can be found as far as <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Jandial" title="Jandial">Jandial temple</a> near <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>. Several examples of <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capitals</a> displaying Ionic influences can be seen as far as <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a>, especially with the <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra_capital" title="Pataliputra capital">Pataliputra capital</a>, dated to the 3rd century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a> is also heavily represented in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">art of Gandhara</a>, especially through <a href="/wiki/Indo-Corinthian_capital" title="Indo-Corinthian capital">Indo-Corinthian capitals</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_Rome">Influence on Rome</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/220px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/330px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/440px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1711" data-file-height="1800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This medallion was produced in <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Rome">Imperial Rome</a>, demonstrating the influence of Alexander's memory. <a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander and his exploits were admired by many Romans, especially generals, who wanted to associate themselves with his achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> began his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">Histories</a></i> by reminding Romans of Alexander's achievements, and thereafter Roman leaders saw him as a role model. <a href="/wiki/Pompey_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Pompey the Great">Pompey the Great</a> adopted the epithet "Magnus" and even Alexander's anastole-type haircut, and searched the conquered lands of the east for Alexander's 260-year-old cloak, which he then wore as a sign of greatness.<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> dedicated a <a href="/wiki/Lysippus" class="mw-redirect" title="Lysippus">Lysippean</a> <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statue" title="Equestrian statue">equestrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> statue but replaced Alexander's head with his own, while <a href="/wiki/Octavian" class="mw-redirect" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> visited Alexander's tomb in Alexandria and temporarily changed his seal from a <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinx</a> to Alexander's profile.<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> The emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> also admired Alexander, as did <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_248-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> The Macriani, a Roman family that in the person of <a href="/wiki/Macrinus" title="Macrinus">Macrinus</a> briefly ascended to the imperial throne, kept images of Alexander on their persons, either on jewelry, or embroidered into their clothes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolt20033_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolt20033-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:203px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg" decoding="async" width="201" height="191" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="201" data-file-height="191" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria" title="Demetrius I of Bactria">Demetrius</a> (reigned <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;200</span>&#160;‚Äì c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;180&#160;BC</span>), wearing an elephant scalp, took over Alexander's legacy in the east by again invading <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and establishing the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Greek kingdom">Indo-Greek kingdom</a> (180&#160;BC‚Äì10&#160;AD).</div></div></div>
<p>On the other hand, some Roman writers, particularly Republican figures, used Alexander as a cautionary tale of how <a href="/wiki/Autocratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Autocratic">autocratic</a> tendencies can be kept in check by <a href="/wiki/Roman_republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman republic">republican</a> values.<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Alexander was used by these writers as an example of ruler values such as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">amicita</i> (friendship) and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">clementia</i> (clemency), but also <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">iracundia</i> (anger) and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">cupiditas gloriae</i> (over-desire for glory).<sup id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unsuccessful_plan_to_cut_a_canal_through_the_isthmus">Unsuccessful plan to cut a canal through the isthmus</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> writes that Alexander wanted to dig the Mimas mountain (today at the <a href="/wiki/Karaburun" title="Karaburun">Karaburun</a> area), but he didn't succeed. He also mentions that this was the only unsuccessful project of Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> writes about this unsuccessful plan adding that the distance was 12 kilometres (<span class="frac nowrap">7<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>&#160;mi), and the purpose was to cut a canal through the isthmus, so as to connect the Caystrian and Hermaean bays.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legend">Legend</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_legend" title="Alexander the Great in legend">Alexander the Great in legend</a></div>
<p>Legendary accounts surround the life of Alexander the Great, many deriving from his own lifetime, probably encouraged by Alexander himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> His court historian Callisthenes portrayed the sea in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> as drawing back from him in proskynesis. Writing shortly after Alexander's death, another participant, <a href="/wiki/Onesicritus" title="Onesicritus">Onesicritus</a>, invented a <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">tryst</a> between Alexander and <a href="/wiki/Thalestris" title="Thalestris">Thalestris</a>, queen of the mythical <a href="/wiki/Amazons" title="Amazons">Amazons</a>. When Onesicritus read this passage to his patron, Alexander's general and later King <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a> reportedly quipped, "I wonder where I was at the time."<sup id="cite_ref-PA46_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA46-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first centuries after Alexander's death, probably in Alexandria, a quantity of the legendary material coalesced into a text known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i>, later falsely ascribed to Callisthenes and therefore known as <i>Pseudo-Callisthenes</i>. This text underwent numerous expansions and revisions throughout Antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> containing many dubious stories,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> and was translated into numerous languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_ancient_and_modern_culture">In ancient and modern culture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great">Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_the_Quran" title="Alexander the Great in the Quran">Alexander the Great in the Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/440px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1225" data-file-height="884" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander the Great depicted in a 14th-century Byzantine manuscript</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_(Venice,_San_Lazzaro,_424)_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/220px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/330px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/440px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="857" data-file-height="1181" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_(Venice,_San_Lazzaro,_424)_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander the Great depicted in a 14th-century Armenian miniature painting</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Submerge2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/220px-Submerge2.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/330px-Submerge2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/440px-Submerge2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1950" data-file-height="1438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Submerge2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic painting</a> depicting Alexander the Great being lowered in a glass <a href="/wiki/Submersible" title="Submersible">submersible</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/220px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/330px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/440px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="737" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The coronation of Alexander depicted in medieval European style in the 15th century romance <i><a href="/wiki/Peniarth_481" title="Peniarth 481">The History of Alexander's Battles</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander the Great's accomplishments and legacy have been depicted in many cultures. Alexander has figured in both high and popular culture beginning in his own era to the present day. The <i>Alexander Romance</i>, in particular, has had a significant impact on portrayals of Alexander in later cultures, from Persian to medieval European to modern Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander features prominently in modern Greek folklore, more so than any other ancient figure.<sup id="cite_ref-Fermor_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> The colloquial form of his name in modern Greek ("O Megalexandros") is a household name, and he is the only ancient hero to appear in the <a href="/wiki/Karagiozis" title="Karagiozis">Karagiozis</a> shadow play.<sup id="cite_ref-Fermor_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> One well-known fable among Greek seamen involves a solitary <a href="/wiki/Mermaid" title="Mermaid">mermaid</a> who would grasp a ship's prow during a storm and ask the captain "Is King Alexander alive?" The correct answer is "He is alive and well and rules the world!" causing the mermaid to vanish and the sea to calm. Any other answer would cause the mermaid to turn into a raging <a href="/wiki/Gorgon" title="Gorgon">Gorgon</a> who would drag the ship to the bottom of the sea, all hands aboard.<sup id="cite_ref-Fermor_258-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Persian language">Middle Persian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a>) literature, Alexander is referred to by the epithet <i>gujastak</i>, meaning "accursed", and is accused of destroying temples and burning the sacred texts of Zoroastrianism.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> In Sunni Islamic Persia, under the influence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i> (in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">ÿßÿ≥⁄©ŸÜÿØÿ±ŸÜÿßŸÖŸá</span>&#8206; <i><a href="/wiki/Iskandarnamah" class="mw-redirect" title="Iskandarnamah">Iskandarnamah</a></i>), a more positive portrayal of Alexander emerges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Firdausi" class="mw-redirect" title="Firdausi">Firdausi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> ("The Book of Kings") includes Alexander in a line of legitimate Persian <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shahs</a>, a mythical figure who explored the far reaches of the world in search of the <a href="/wiki/Fountain_of_Youth" title="Fountain of Youth">Fountain of Youth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Later Persian writers associate him with philosophy, portraying him at a symposium with figures such as <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and Aristotle, in search of immortality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The figure of <a href="/wiki/Dhul-Qarnayn" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhul-Qarnayn">Dhul-Qarnayn</a> (literally "the Two-Horned One") mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> is believed by scholars to be based on later legends of Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_260-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In this tradition, he was a heroic figure who built a wall to defend against the nations of <a href="/wiki/Gog_and_Magog" title="Gog and Magog">Gog and Magog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> He then travelled the known world in search of the Water of Life and Immortality, eventually becoming a prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> version of the <i>Alexander Romance</i> portrays him as an ideal Christian world conqueror who prayed to "the one true God".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_260-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In Egypt, Alexander was portrayed as the son of <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> before the Persian conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_262-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> His defeat of Darius was depicted as Egypt's salvation, "proving" Egypt was still ruled by an Egyptian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_260-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, Alexander was shown the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a> when he entered Jerusalem, which described a mighty Greek king who would conquer the Persian Empire. This is cited as a reason for sparing Jerusalem.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, the name "Sikandar", derived from the Persian name for Alexander, denotes a rising young talent, and the Delhi Sultanate ruler <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Aladdin Khajli</a> stylized himself as "Sikandar-i-Sani" (the Second Alexander the Great).<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Medieval_India" title="Medieval India">medieval India</a>, Turkic and Afghan sovereigns from the Iranian-cultured region of Central Asia brought positive cultural connotations of Alexander to the Indian subcontinent, resulting in the efflorescence of <i>Sikandernameh</i> (<a href="/wiki/Alexander_romance" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander romance">Alexander Romances</a>) written by Indo-Persian poets such as <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrow" class="mw-redirect" title="Amir Khusrow">Amir Khusrow</a> and the prominence of Alexander the Great as a popular subject in Mughal-era Persian miniatures.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a>, Alexander the Great was revered as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Worthies" title="Nine Worthies">Nine Worthies</a>, a group of heroes whose lives were believed to encapsulate all the ideal qualities of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_Anthology" title="Greek Anthology">Greek Anthology</a> there are poems referring to Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Irish playwright <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Thomas_de_Vere" title="Aubrey Thomas de Vere">Aubrey Thomas de Vere</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great,_a_Dramatic_Poem" title="Alexander the Great, a Dramatic Poem">Alexander the Great, a Dramatic Poem</a></i>.
</p><p>In popular culture, the British heavy metal band <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a> included a song titled "Alexander the Great" on their 1986 album <i><a href="/wiki/Somewhere_in_Time_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Somewhere in Time (Iron Maiden album)">Somewhere in Time</a></i>. Written by bass player <a href="/wiki/Steve_Harris_(musician)" title="Steve Harris (musician)">Steve Harris</a>, the song retells Alexander's life.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Historiography of Alexander the Great">Historiography of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Apart from a few inscriptions and fragments, texts written by people who actually knew Alexander or who gathered information from men who served with Alexander were all lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Contemporaries who wrote accounts of his life included Alexander's campaign historian Callisthenes; Alexander's generals Ptolemy and <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>; <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a>, a junior officer on the campaigns; and Onesicritus, Alexander's chief helmsman. Their works are lost, but later works based on these <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">original sources</a> have survived. The earliest of these is <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> (1st century BC), followed by Quintus Curtius Rufus (mid-to-late 1st century AD), Arrian (1st to 2nd century AD), the biographer Plutarch (1st to 2nd century AD), and finally <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a>, whose work dated as late as the 4th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Of these, Arrian is generally considered the most reliable, given that he used Ptolemy and Aristobulus as his sources, closely followed by Diodorus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Annotations">Annotations</span></h2>
<div style=""><ol class="references" style="list-style-type:upper-alpha;">
<li id="cnote_a"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_a_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Macedon was an Ancient Greek polity. The Macedonians were a Greek tribe. Historiography and scholarship agree that Alexander the Great was Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-Macedonians_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macedonians-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> </span></li>
<li id="cnote_b"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_b_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> By the time of his death, he had conquered the entire <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Persian Empire</a>, adding it to Macedon's European territories; according to some modern writers, this was most of the world then known to the ancient Greeks (the '<a href="/wiki/Ecumene#Ancient_world" title="Ecumene">Ecumene</a>').<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738,_49,_167_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738,_49,_167-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> An approximate view of the world known to Alexander can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a>'s map; see <a href="/wiki/File:Hecataeus_world_map-en.svg" title="File:Hecataeus world map-en.svg">Hecataeus world map</a>. </span></li>
<li id="cnote_c"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_c_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> supposedly ranked Alexander as the greatest general;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327‚Äì28_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327‚Äì28-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Julius Caesar wept on seeing a statue of Alexander, since he had achieved so little by the same age;<sup id="cite_ref-Plutarch,_Caesar,_11_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch,_Caesar,_11-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> consciously posed as the 'new Alexander';<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176‚Äì83_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176‚Äì83-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> the young <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> also encouraged comparisons with Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> </span></li>
<li id="cnote_d"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_d_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The name <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ºàŒªŒ≠ŒæŒ±ŒΩŒ¥œÅŒøœÇ</span> derives from the Greek verb <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ºÄŒªŒ≠Œæœâ</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">al√©x≈ç</i>, <a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;'ward off, avert, defend'</span>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919&#91;httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29le2Fcw_IV,_57&#93;_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29le2Fcw_IV,_57]-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ºÄŒΩŒ¥œÅ-</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">andr-</i>), the stem of <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ºÄŒΩŒÆœÅ</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization">an·∏ór</i>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;'man'</span>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919&#91;httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29nh2Fr_IV,_57&#93;_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpwwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29nh2Fr_IV,_57]-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> and means "protector of men".<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> </span></li>
<li id="cnote_e"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_e_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> There have been, since the time, many suspicions that Pausanias was actually hired to murder Philip. Suspicion has fallen upon Alexander, Olympias and even the newly crowned Persian Emperor, Darius III. All three of these people had motive to have Philip murdered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane_Fox198072‚Äì73_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane_Fox198072‚Äì73-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> </span></li>
<li id="cnote_f"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_f_1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> However, <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>, who used <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> as a source, said that Alexander crossed with more than 5,000 horse and 30,000 foot; <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> quoted the same totals, but listed 5,100 horse and 32,000 foot. Diodorus also referred to an advance force already present in Asia, which <a href="/wiki/Polyaenus" title="Polyaenus">Polyaenus</a>, in his Stratagems of War (5.44.4), said numbered 10,000 men.</span></li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208_221-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFOgden2009">Ogden 2009</a>, p.&#160;208...&#160;three attested pregnancies in eight years produces an attested impregnation rate of one every 2.7&#160;years, which is actually superior to that of his father.</span>

<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Mary_Renault" title="Mary Renault">Mary Renault</a> (1979). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nature_of_Alexander" title="The Nature of Alexander">The Nature of Alexander</a></i>. Pantheon. p.&#160;110. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0394738253" title="Special:BookSources/978-0394738253"><bdi>978-0394738253</bdi></a>. <q>No record at all exists of such a woman [ie, Barsine] accompanying his march; nor of any claim by her, or her powerful kin, that she had borne him offspring. Yet twelve years after his death a boy was produced, seventeen years old, born therefore five years after Damascus, her alleged son "brought up in Pergamon"; a claimant and shortlived pawn in the succession wars, chosen probably for a physical resemblance to Alexander. That he actually did marry another Barsine must have helped both to launch and preserve the story; but no source reports any notice whatever taken by him of a child who, Roxane's being posthumous, would have been during his lifetime his only son, by a near-royal mother. In a man who named cities after his horse and dog, this strains credulity.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Nature+of+Alexander&amp;rft.pages=110&amp;rft.pub=Pantheon&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.isbn=978-0394738253&amp;rft.au=Mary+Renault&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-livius-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-livius_229-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-livius_229-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.livius.org/aj-al/alexander/alexander_z2.html">"Alexander the Great: his towns"</a>. livius.org<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Burn-230"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Burn_230-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burn_230-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burn_230-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burn_230-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFBurn2004" class="citation book">Burn, Lucilla (2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=TmhjC_AdoNsC&amp;pg=PA10"><i>Hellenistic Art: From Alexander the Great to Augustus</i></a>. London, England: The British Museum Press. pp.&#160;10‚Äì11. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-89236-776-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-89236-776-4"><bdi>978-0-89236-776-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hellenistic+Art%3A+From+Alexander+the+Great+to+Augustus&amp;rft.place=London%2C+England&amp;rft.pages=10-11&amp;rft.pub=The+British+Museum+Press&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-89236-776-4&amp;rft.aulast=Burn&amp;rft.aufirst=Lucilla&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DTmhjC_AdoNsC%26pg%3DPA10&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BritishMuseumTumblr-231"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BritishMuseumTumblr_231-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BritishMuseumTumblr_231-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://britishmuseum.tumblr.com/post/121260555272/alexander-the-great">"Alexander the Great"</a>. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Alexander+the+Great&amp;rft.pub=British+Museum&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbritishmuseum.tumblr.com%2Fpost%2F121260555272%2Falexander-the-great&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/> "On reaching Priene, he made a further dedication to Athena. There the townspeople were laying out their new city and building a temple to its patron goddess. Alexander offered funds to complete the temple, and the inscription on this wall block, cut into a block of marble, records his gift. The inscription was found in the 19th century by the architect-archaeologist Richard Pullan leading an expedition on behalf of the Society of Dilettanti. It reads: 'King Alexander dedicated the Temple to Athena Polias'."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BritishMuseum-232"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BritishMuseum_232-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BritishMuseum_232-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.britishmuseum.org/research/collection_online/collection_object_details.aspx?objectId=437260&amp;partId=1&amp;searchText=athena+polias&amp;people=72426&amp;page=1">"Collection online"</a>. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Collection+online&amp;rft.pub=British+Museum&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.britishmuseum.org%2Fresearch%2Fcollection_online%2Fcollection_object_details.aspx%3FobjectId%3D437260%26partId%3D1%26searchText%3Dathena%2Bpolias%26people%3D72426%26page%3D1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/> "Marble wall block from the temple of Athena at Priene, inscribed on two sides. The inscription on the front records the gift of funds from Alexander the Great to complete the temple."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-233"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-233">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.britishmuseum.org/research/collection_online/collection_object_details/collection_image_gallery.aspx?partid=1&amp;assetid=457544001&amp;objectid=437260">"Priene Inscription"</a>. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Priene+Inscription&amp;rft.pub=British+Museum&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.britishmuseum.org%2Fresearch%2Fcollection_online%2Fcollection_object_details%2Fcollection_image_gallery.aspx%3Fpartid%3D1%26assetid%3D457544001%26objectid%3D437260&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/> "Marble wall block from the temple of Athena at Priene, inscribed.
